<div>
<strong>Previous version available:</strong> This article is based on a previously available preprint with doi: <a href="https://doi.org/10.1101/2022.11.23.517700" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://doi.org/10.1101/2022.11.23.517700</a>.</div>
<div>See commentary "<a href="/articles/PMC10468202/" class="usa-link">Orchestrating pH levels in plants</a>", e91025.</div>
</div>
</div></section></section><section aria-label="Article content"><section class="body main-article-body"><section class="abstract" id="abstract1"><h2>Abstract</h2>
<p>Plant roots navigate in the soil environment following the gravity vector. Cell divisions in the meristem and rapid cell growth in the elongation zone propel the root tips through the soil. Actively elongating cells acidify their apoplast to enable cell wall extension by the activity of plasma membrane AHA H<sup>+</sup>-ATPases. The phytohormone auxin, central regulator of gravitropic response and root development, inhibits root cell growth, likely by rising the pH of the apoplast. However, the role of auxin in the regulation of the apoplastic pH gradient along the root tip is unclear. Here, we show, by using an improved method for visualization and quantification of root surface pH, that the <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> root surface pH shows distinct acidic and alkaline zones, which are not primarily determined by the activity of AHA H<sup>+</sup>-ATPases. Instead, the distinct domain of alkaline pH in the root transition zone is controlled by a rapid auxin response module, consisting of the AUX1 auxin influx carrier, the AFB1 auxin co-receptor, and the CNCG14 calcium channel. We demonstrate that the rapid auxin response pathway is required for an efficient navigation of the root tip.</p>
<section id="kwd-group2" class="kwd-group"><p><strong>Research organism:</strong> <em>A. thaliana</em></p></section></section><section id="s1"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Introduction</h2>
<p>Plants colonize soil by the growth of their roots. Because plant cells are non-motile, mechanically coupled by their cell walls and symplastically connected by plasmodesmata, the extent and direction of root growth is driven by the elongation of cells that can differ across the organ (<a href="#bib9" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib9">Braidwood et al., 2014</a>). The balance between cell proliferation and differentiation in the root apex is controlled by several phytohormone and peptide signaling pathways, among which auxin plays a specific role, thanks to its long-distance intercellular transport that serves as the information carrier between distant plant tissues (<a href="#bib26" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib26">Grieneisen et al., 2007</a>; <a href="#bib77" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib77">Wisniewska et al., 2006</a>).</p>
<p>The root apex is divided in several zones fulfilling various roles in root development. At the tip of the root, the root cap covers and protects the meristem, and is the center of gravity perception. In the meristem, stem cells produce transit-amplifying cells that populate the root tip (<a href="#bib16" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib16">Dolan et al., 1993</a>; <a href="#bib11" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib11">Campilho et al., 2006</a>). In the model plant <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>, the root cap reaches up to the transition zone, where cells stop dividing and prepare for elongation; the transition zone is characterized by a distinct physiology and shows a high responsiveness to external factors (<a href="#bib73" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib73">Verbelen et al., 2006</a>). Further in the shootward direction, cells rapidly elongate in the elongation zone, propelling the root tip through the soil (<a href="#bib4" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib4">Beemster and Baskin, 1998</a>). After reaching their final length, cells differentiate in the maturation zone, where the root hairs emerge presumably to increase the absorptive root surface.</p>
<p>To elongate, cells must expand their cell walls, increase turgor pressure or both (<a href="#bib37" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib37">Lintilhac, 2014</a>). Proton extrusion into the apoplast by the plasma membrane (PM) H<sup>+</sup> ATPases is crucial for both cell wall expansion and turgor maintenance. Acidic pH leads to activation of cell wall remodeling enzymes, and the H<sup>+</sup> gradient at the PM drives the transport of most nutrients and ions, and as such is required for turgor pressure generation (<a href="#bib21" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib21">Falhof et al., 2016</a>). It is therefore not surprising that root zones of numerous species show a distinct pattern of surface proton fluxes and cell wall pH (<a href="#bib67" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib67">Siao et al., 2020</a>). In general, the tip of the root and the late elongation and maturation zones show outward H<sup>+</sup> fluxes, while the transition zone and early elongation zones show inward H<sup>+</sup> fluxes (<a href="#bib76" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib76">Weisenseel and Meyer, 1997</a>). Several studies showed that this battery-like system, with positive charges flowing out of the maturation zone and entering at the transition zone, is mainly driven by H<sup>+</sup> fluxes without completely excluding calcium and chloride ions (<a href="#bib74" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib74">Weisenseel et al., 1979</a>; <a href="#bib8" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib8">Björkman and Leopold, 1987</a>; <a href="#bib6" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib6">Behrens et al., 1982</a>; <a href="#bib32" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib32">Iwabuchi et al., 1989</a>). Similar pattern of H<sup>+</sup> fluxes was determined in <em>A. thaliana</em>, where the fluxes also correlated with root surface pH: an alkaline domain was observed at the border between meristematic and transition zones (<a href="#bib69" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib69">Staal et al., 2011</a>). Other works have attempted to determine the pH of the epidermal cell walls, and show a general decrease of pH toward the maturation zone of the root (<a href="#bib3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib3">Barbez et al., 2017</a>; <a href="#bib47" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib47">Moreau et al., 2022</a>; <a href="#bib27" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib27">Großeholz et al., 2022</a>). It is important to keep in mind that changes in proton fluxes and membrane potential are not always correlated with change in pH. For example, a simultaneous leak of protons and uptake through H<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> symporter in the cytoplasm can lead to apoplast acidification with a relatively stable membrane potential (<a href="#bib70" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib70">Stanković, 2006</a>). In general, the relationship between H<sup>+</sup> fluxes, cell wall pH, and the growth rate in the particular zones remains unclear: the highest pH is observed in the transition and early elongation zones, while the lowest pH and highest outward H<sup>+</sup> fluxes are observed in the maturation zone where cells do not elongate. In particular, the mechanism of the alkaline domain formation and its physiological significance remain unclear.</p>
<p>The apoplastic pH is largely controlled by the activity of the proton pump H<sup>+</sup> ATPases, encoded by 11 genes in the <em>A. thaliana</em> genome; in roots, the dominant paralogs are AHA1 and AHA2 (<a href="#bib29" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib29">Haruta et al., 2010</a>). While their activity is mainly regulated by phosphorylation (<a href="#bib21" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib21">Falhof et al., 2016</a>), the expression and membrane localization of AHAs along the longitudinal root axis seems to be important for the zonation of the <em>A. thaliana</em> root. <a href="#bib31" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib31">Haruta et al., 2018</a>, showed that under conditions of dim light, the AHA2-mCitrine was less abundant in the membranes of transition zone cells, and this partially correlated with higher surface pH of the transition zone cells. Under conditions of light, the AHA2-mCitrine localizes to the membranes in the transition zone. <a href="#bib51" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib51">Pacifici et al., 2018</a>, presented a model where cytokinin signaling regulates root meristem size by controlling the expression of AHA1 and AHA2. Increased cytokinin signaling led to shortening of the meristem, similarly to an inducible relocalization of AHA2 fused to the glucocorticoid receptor. Finally, <a href="#bib27" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib27">Großeholz et al., 2022</a>, quantified the AHA2-GFP signal and demonstrated an increasing AHA2-GFP membrane abundance toward the maturation zone of <em>A. thaliana</em> root, which correlated with increased acidification of the root surface. Still, the observed patterns of proton fluxes along the root zones (<a href="#bib76" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib76">Weisenseel and Meyer, 1997</a>), and in particular the existence of the alkaline domain around the transition zone of the root, cannot be simply explained by the expression or abundance of AHA proton pumps.</p>
<p>Roots use the gravity vector as the reference for the direction of their growth. A general pattern was shown in various plant species: during the gravitropic response, ion fluxes change rapidly, leading to increased proton secretion on the upper and decreased secretion on the lower side, where it correlates with growth inhibition, and results in bending of the root (<a href="#bib48" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib48">Mulkey and Evans, 1981</a>; <a href="#bib6" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib6">Behrens et al., 1982</a>; <a href="#bib32" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib32">Iwabuchi et al., 1989</a>; <a href="#bib13" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib13">Collings et al., 1992</a>; <a href="#bib75" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib75">Weisenseel et al., 1992</a>). In <em>A. thaliana</em>, the root surface and cell wall alkalinization of lower side upon gravistimulation has also been clearly demonstrated (<a href="#bib46" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib46">Monshausen et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#bib66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib66">Shih et al., 2015</a>; <a href="#bib3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib3">Barbez et al., 2017</a>), and it was shown that this alkalinization requires functional auxin transport (<a href="#bib46" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib46">Monshausen et al., 2011</a>) and the CNGC14 calcium channel acting downstream of auxin signaling (<a href="#bib66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib66">Shih et al., 2015</a>). Alkalinization of the lower root side is thus triggered by the redirection of auxin flux toward the lateral root cap and epidermal cells on the lower side of the root (<a href="#bib10" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib10">Brunoud et al., 2012</a>). Analogously, apoplast alkalinization can be triggered by the application of auxin to the roots (<a href="#bib19" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib19">Evans et al., 1980</a>; <a href="#bib41" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib41">Lüthen and Böttger, 1988</a>; <a href="#bib46" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib46">Monshausen et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#bib66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib66">Shih et al., 2015</a>; <a href="#bib3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib3">Barbez et al., 2017</a>). The root surface pH increases almost immediately upon auxin application, and this process requires the activity of the CNGC14 calcium channel; in the mutant, surface alkalinization as well as the auxin-induced growth inhibition are delayed (<a href="#bib66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib66">Shih et al., 2015</a>). The rapid root growth inhibition as well as the membrane depolarization and surface alkalinization triggered by auxin depend on the rapid branch of the TIR1/AFB signaling branch; auxin influx by AUX1 and the AFB1 auxin co-receptor paralogue play a prominent role in the rapid auxin response (<a href="#bib22" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib22">Fendrych et al., 2018</a>; <a href="#bib15" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib15">Dindas et al., 2018</a>; <a href="#bib52" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib52">Prigge et al., 2020</a>; <a href="#bib63" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib63">Serre et al., 2021</a>; <a href="#bib35" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib35">Li et al., 2021</a>; <a href="#bib17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib17">Dubey et al., 2021</a>). The molecular mechanism of surface alkalinization is, however, not understood. It was proposed that auxin plays a dual role in regulation of root apoplastic pH: auxin alkalinizes the apoplast by the activity of an unknown ion channel downstream of the TIR1/AFB signaling, and, at the same time, auxin upregulates the activity of AHA H<sup>+</sup> ATPases through TMK1 signaling (<a href="#bib35" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib35">Li et al., 2021</a>). It is not clear how auxin activates the TMK kinases. On the other hand, the connection between TIR1/AFB auxin signaling and activity of AHAs has been clarified: the auxin-induced SAUR proteins inhibit the PP2C-D clade of protein phosphatases that act as negative regulators of AHAs, leading to the activation of proton secretion. This pathway was studied mainly in shoots, but seems also to operate in roots, as the <em>Arabidopsis</em> lines with manipulated SAUR or PP2C-D expression show root phenotypes that are consistent with the expected changes in AHA activities (<a href="#bib68" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib68">Spartz et al., 2014</a>; <a href="#bib55" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib55">Ren et al., 2018</a>). In summary, the connection between auxin signaling, regulation of the activity of AHA H<sup>+</sup> ATPases, and the longitudinal zonation of the root surface pH profile during normal growth and during gravitropic responses remains unclear.</p>
<p>Here, by employing an improved method for visualization and quantification of root surface pH, we focus on the molecular actors involved in the establishment of the longitudinal surface pH zonation. In particular, we show that the alkaline domain around the transition zone is not caused by the lack of AHA H<sup>+</sup> ATPases activity. Further, we demonstrate that the alkaline domain at the transition zone is controlled by the components of the rapid auxin response pathway. Finally, we address the significance of the dynamic surface pH profile for the gravitropic response of the roots.</p></section><section id="s2"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Results</h2>
<section id="s2-1"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">The <em>Arabidopsis</em> root shows distinct acidic and alkaline root surface pH domains</h3>
<p>To monitor the spatio-temporal dynamics of root ion fluxes and apoplastic pH in vertically growing roots of <em>A. thaliana</em>, we re-evaluated the available fluorescence staining methods to visualize pH in roots. Cell wall staining by 8-hydroxypyrene-1,3,6-trisulfonic acid trisodium salt (HPTS) (<a href="#bib3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib3">Barbez et al., 2017</a>) was not satisfactory in our setup, due to the very high background signal and probably due to the absence of the optimal 458 nm excitation in our vertical stage spinning disk microscope (<a href="#bib63" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib63">Serre et al., 2021</a>). However, at the transition zone of the root, we could observe an alkaline domain on the root surface (<a href="#fig1s1" class="usa-link">Figure 1—figure supplement 1a</a>). Further, we attempted to visualize root surface pH using Fluorescein Dextran and Oregon Green Dextran (<a href="#fig1s1" class="usa-link">Figure 1—figure supplement 1a</a>) pH reporters (<a href="#bib46" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib46">Monshausen et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#bib25" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib25">Geilfus and Mühling, 2011</a>), but the results were not satisfactory due to an artifact when roots were imaged on solid medium (<a href="#fig1s1" class="usa-link">Figure 1—figure supplement 1d</a>). We therefore searched for alternative pH-sensitive fluorescent dyes, and discovered Fluorescein-5-(and-6)-Sulfonic Acid, Trisodium Salt (FS) (Invitrogen F1130; <a href="#bib62" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib62">Seksek et al., 1995</a>; <a href="#bib57" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib57">Rosario and Rojas, 1986</a>) as an excellent reporter of root surface pH.</p>
<p>The F<sub>488/405</sub> excitation ratio of FS efficiently reported pH when dissolved in liquid or solid medium (<a href="#fig1" class="usa-link">Figure 1a–d</a>, <a href="#fig1s1" class="usa-link">Figure 1—figure supplement 1b,c</a>). The F<sub>488/405</sub> excitation ratio of FS is insensitive to the redox state of the medium but influenced by cation concentration (<a href="#fig1s1" class="usa-link">Figure 1—figure supplement 1e</a>). FS allows to visualize pH of the root surface and the surrounding rhizosphere, without penetrating the root tissues (<a href="#fig1" class="usa-link">Figure 1e</a>). The pH imaging with FS highlighted the previously described relatively alkaline domain in the transition zone/early elongation zone of the root (<a href="#bib46" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib46">Monshausen et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#bib69" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib69">Staal et al., 2011</a>). In addition, we observed two relatively acidic pH domains, one located in the late elongation/root hair zone and the other in the proximity of the root tip (<a href="#fig1" class="usa-link">Figure 1e</a>). To unbiasedly quantify the root surface pH, we developed a Python-based program to determine the F<sub>488/405</sub> ratio of the root surface (<a href="#fig1s1" class="usa-link">Figure 1—figure supplement 1f</a>). The quantification highlighted the presence of the observed relatively alkaline and acidic domains (<a href="#fig1" class="usa-link">Figure 1f</a>). Throughout the text, we will refer to these domains as alkaline and acidic domains, by which we mean values relatively acidic or relatively alkaline in comparison with the pH of the medium. The surface pH profile of roots shown by FS is in agreement with the data obtained using numerous electrode and microscopy measurements in several species including <em>A. thaliana</em> (<a href="#bib79" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib79">Zieschang et al., 1993</a>; <a href="#bib69" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib69">Staal et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#bib45" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib45">Monshausen et al., 1996</a>; <a href="#bib76" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib76">Weisenseel and Meyer, 1997</a>). The FS pH detection range thus reveals both the alkaline and acidic domains of the root surface and allows direct and dynamic visualization of proton concentration on the root surface and its close surroundings.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="fig1"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 1. Fluorescein-5-(and-6)-Sulfonic Acid, Trisodium Salt (FS) dye reveals acidic and alkaline domains at the root root surface.</h4>
<figcaption><p>(<strong>a, b</strong>) The pH dependence of the excitation (<strong>a</strong>) and emission (<strong>b</strong>) spectrum of FS in liquid plant growth medium. Excitation spectra were recorded at λEm = 520 nm, emission spectra were excited by λEx = 488 nm. (<strong>c</strong>) FS fluorescence in solid agar medium at indicated pH values, fluorescence excited by 488 nm, 405 nm and the F<sub>488/405</sub> excitation ratio, LUT as in (<strong>e</strong>). (<strong>d</strong>) Quantification of the F<sub>488/405</sub> excitation ratio in (<strong>c</strong>). (<strong>e</strong>) <em>A. thaliana</em> root tip shows the alkaline and acidic surface pH domains, arrow indicates the gravity vector, scale bar = 50 µm. The color of the root itself does reflect pH, as the root itself is not stained by FS (see <a href="#fig1s1" class="usa-link">Figure 1—figure supplement 1f</a>). The pink line shows the region in which F488/405 excitation ratio was plotted in (<strong>f</strong>). (<strong>f</strong>) The F<sub>488/405</sub> excitation ratio of FS along the longitudinal axis of the root. In (<strong>e, f</strong>), the typical root zones are depicted for illustration. The source data can be found in <a href="#fig1sdata1" class="usa-link">Figure 1—source data 1</a>.</p>

For all dyes, the reference excitation channel (405 nm) and the pH-responsive excitation channel (488 nm) are shown. HPTS shows an alkaline domain at the transition zone, while Fluorescein Dextran and Oregon Green Dextran accumulate in a ‘pocket’ (see (<strong>d</strong>)), preventing the observation of longitudinal pH profile. The root surface is automatically recognized, creating a defined region of interest (ROI) in a 10-pixel distance from the surface. Fluorescence intensities in 488 and 405 nm excitation channels are measured in the ROIs. The data is binned and the FS F<sub>488/405</sub> excitation ratio is calculated as a function of distance from the root tip. Scale bar = 100 µm. <div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/fig1/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></figure></section><section id="s2-2"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">The alkaline domain in the transition zone is not directly determined by AHA activation or localization</h3>
<p>We first hypothesized that the observed spatial surface pH gradients might originate from gradients of AHA ATPase abundance or activity (<a href="#bib27" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib27">Großeholz et al., 2022</a>). We therefore immunolocalized endogenous AHAs using the antibody which recognizes multiple AHA ATPases paralogs in multiple plant species (Agrisera AS07260). To test the specificity of the antibody in <em>Arabidopsis</em> roots, we imaged Col-0 roots, <em>aha2-4</em> mutants lacking the dominant AHA2 paralogue, and lines inducibly expressing a dominant version of AHA2 (AHA2-d95-mScarlet). The antibody showed signal in the PM in Col-0 and <em>aha2-4</em> roots (<a href="#fig2s1" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 1a and b</a>). When we inducibly overexpressed the AHA2-d95-mScarlet, we detected colocalization of the mScarlet signal with the antibody signal (<a href="#fig2s1" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 1c</a>). These results indicate that the antibody recognized multiple plasma membrane localized AHAs in <em>Arabidopsis</em> roots. The immunolocalization of endogenous AHAs, however, didn’t reveal any obvious absence of AHAs in the transition zone (<a href="#fig2" class="usa-link">Figure 2a</a>, <a href="#fig2s1" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 1d</a>) that would explain the presence of the alkaline domain. To overcome the hypothetical lack of AHA activation in the TZ, we treated seedlings with fusicoccin (FC), a fungal toxin that stimulates the AHA’s activity (<a href="#bib1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib1">Ballio et al., 1964</a>) and thus increases proton efflux. FC lowered the surface pH of the root acidic domains but, surprisingly, did not affect the alkaline domain (<a href="#fig2" class="usa-link">Figure 2b and c</a>). This partial acidification was correlated with the known FC-induced stimulation of root growth (<a href="#fig2s2" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 2a</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="fig2"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 2. The alkaline domain does not directly depend on proton pump activity.</h4>
<figcaption><p>(<strong>a</strong>) Immunostaining of AHA proton pumps in the Col-0 root transition zone (arrowhead). Scale bar = 20 µm. (<strong>b,c</strong>) Root surface pH visualized by FS of wild-type (WT) Col-0 seedlings treated with 0 µM (control) or 2 µM fusicoccin. (<strong>b</strong>) Representative image (scale bar = 100 µm) and (<strong>c</strong>) quantifications of FS F488/405 excitation ratio profile. (<strong>d</strong>) Image of root tips of the tissue-specific inducible lines PIN2 &gt;&gt;AHA2-Δ95-mScarlet and PIN2 &gt;&gt;PP2C-D1-mScarlet seedlings after 4 hr induction by 5 µM estradiol. Scale bar = 50 µm. (<strong>e,f</strong>) Root surface pH visualized by FS of WT Col-0 and induced PIN2 &gt;&gt;AHA2-Δ95-mScarlet and PIN2 &gt;&gt;PP2C-D1-mScarlet lines. (<strong>e</strong>) Representative images (scale bar = 100 µm), and (<strong>f</strong>) quantifications of FS F488/405 excitation ratio profile. For (<strong>c</strong>) and (<strong>f</strong>), the bars above the curves mark regions with non-significant (ns) and significant (*: p-value &lt;0.05) statistical difference in comparison to control. The source data can be found in <a href="#fig2sdata1" class="usa-link">Figure 2—source data 1</a>.</p>

Scale bars in all images = 10 µm.</div></figcaption></figure></div>
<div class="p"><figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="fig2s2"><h5 class="obj_head">Figure 2—figure supplement 2. The influence of genetic manipulation of AHAs on root surface pH.</h5>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=10414970_elife-85193-fig2-figsupp2.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/343a/10414970/99d8dfe8e954/elife-85193-fig2-figsupp2.jpg" loading="lazy" height="1087" width="698" alt="Figure 2—figure supplement 2."></a></p>
<figcaption><div class="p">(<strong>a</strong>) Root elongation (µm/min) of Col-0 treated with 0 or 2 µM fusicoccin. Growth was measured over 40 min of imaging. (<strong>b–e</strong>) Roots of Col-0 and PIN2 &gt;&gt;AHA2-Δ95 and PIN2 &gt;&gt;PP2C-D1 lines after 4 hr induction by 5 µM estradiol and 20 min treatment with 0 or 10 nM indole-3-acetic acid (IAA). (<strong>b</strong>) Root elongation (µm/min) measured over 40 min. (<strong>c</strong>) Representative images of pH visualization by FS; scale bar = 100 µm. (<strong>d</strong>) Root surface pH quantification by FS F<sub>488/405</sub> excitation ratio. <div class="p">
<section class="sm xbox font-sm" id="fig2s2sdata1"><div class="caption p"><span>Figure 2—figure supplement 2—source data 1. Data used for generating the graphs in the figure.</span></div>
<div class="media p" id="d64e939"><div class="caption">
<a href="/articles/instance/10414970/bin/elife-85193-fig2-figsupp2-data1.xlsx" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" class="usa-link">elife-85193-fig2-figsupp2-data1.xlsx</a><sup> (318.1KB, xlsx) </sup>
</div></div></section>
</div></figcaption></figure></div>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/fig2/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></figure><p>To exclude an FC-independent regulation of AHAs in the TZ, we created <em>A. thaliana</em> lines inducibly expressing a fluorescently tagged hyperactive version of AHA2 (<a href="#bib50" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib50">Pacheco-Villalobos et al., 2016</a>) - AHA2-d95-mScarlet, and lines expressing an inhibitor of AHAs PP2CD1-mScarlet (<a href="#bib55" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib55">Ren et al., 2018</a>) under the control of the epidermal/cortex PIN2 promoter (<a href="#fig2" class="usa-link">Figure 2d</a>). Expression of hyperactive AHA2 increased the acidic domain in the root tip, slightly reduced the alkaline domain, but did not prevent its formation (<a href="#fig2" class="usa-link">Figure 2e and f</a>). This restricted acidification led to a tendency to stimulate root growth (<a href="#fig2s2" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 2b</a>).</p>
<p>Inhibition of AHA activity by overexpression of PP2C-D1 raised the overall root tip surface pH, but did not prevent the formation of the alkaline domain in the TZ (<a href="#fig2" class="usa-link">Figure 2e and f</a>). Alkalinization of the root tip surface correlated with a statistically insignificant reduction of root growth (<a href="#fig2s2" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 2b</a>). We further measured the root surface pH profile of <em>aha2</em> and <em>pp2c-d triple</em> mutants that showed decreased (<a href="#bib30" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib30">Haruta and Sussman, 2012</a>) and increased (<a href="#bib55" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib55">Ren et al., 2018</a>) AHA activity, respectively. Plants lacking the expression of PP2CDs showed a significantly acidified root tip surface, however, the alkaline halo remained unaffected (<a href="#fig2s2" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 2f and h</a>). On the other hand, plants lacking the expression of AHA2 were slightly impaired in the alkaline halo domain formation (<a href="#fig2s2" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 2f and h</a>). <em>aha2-4</em> roots were growing slower than Col-0 while <em>pp2c-d triple</em> root growth was stimulated (<a href="#fig2s2" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 2g</a>).</p>
<p>The manipulation of the AHA proton pump activity resulted in the expected outcome of influencing the overall root surface pH and root growth. However, the spatial organization of the root surface pH profile with the alkaline domain of the TZ does not appear to be simply controlled by the activation or abundance of AHAs, as the alkaline domain cannot be fully removed by genetic or pharmacological modulation of the proton pumping activity.</p></section><section id="s2-3"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">The establishment of the alkaline pH domain requires AUX1</h3>
<p>It is well established that the application of auxin to roots causes an increase in apoplastic and root surface pH (<a href="#bib46" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib46">Monshausen et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#bib66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib66">Shih et al., 2015</a>; <a href="#bib35" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib35">Li et al., 2021</a>). To investigate the effect of auxin application on surface pH profile and formation of the TZ alkaline domain, we analyzed surface pH in response to the native auxin indole-3-acetic acid (IAA). With the increasing IAA concentration, the alkaline domain pH rose and the domain expanded toward the elongation zone of the root, which resulted in disappearance of the acidic domain (<a href="#fig3" class="usa-link">Figure 3a and b</a>). The extent of the surface alkalinization correlated with IAA-induced root growth inhibition; the response was detectable at 1 nM IAA and was saturated between 100 and 1000 nM IAA (<a href="#fig3" class="usa-link">Figure 3c</a>). This implies that the entire root surface is capable of alkalinization upon external auxin treatment, and that the alkaline domain is the hotspot of auxin response.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="fig3"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 3. Auxin influx by AUX1 is essential for the initiation of the alkaline domain.</h4>
<figcaption><p>(<strong>a–c</strong>) Surface pH correlates with growth rate-dose response of Col-0 roots to indole-3-acetic acid (IAA) auxin. (<strong>a</strong>) Representative images of root surface pH visualized by FS after 20 min IAA treatment. (<strong>b</strong>) Quantifications of FS F488/405 excitation ratio profile. (<strong>c</strong>) Root elongation rate (µm/min) measured over a 40 min period. (<strong>d–f</strong>) Root surface pH visualized by FS of Col-0, <em>aux1</em> and <em>pin2</em> mutants after 20 min treatment with 0 or 100 nM IAA. (<strong>d</strong>) Representative images. (<strong>e</strong>) Quantification of FS F488/405 excitation ratio profile in (<strong>e</strong>) control condition and (<strong>f</strong>) in response to 100 nM IAA. For (b,e,f), the bars above the curves mark regions with non-significant (ns) and significant (*: p-value &lt;0.05) statistical difference in comparison to control. For (c), *: p-value &lt;0.05, ***: p-value &lt;0.0005. Scale bars = 100 µm. The source data can be found in <a href="#fig3sdata1" class="usa-link">Figure 3—source data 1</a>.</p>

<figcaption><div class="p">(<strong>a–c</strong>) Roots of Col-0, <em>aux1</em> and <em>pin2</em> mutants after 20 min treatment with 0 or 10 nM indole-3-acetic acid (IAA). <div class="p">
<section class="sm xbox font-sm" id="fig3s1sdata1"><div class="caption p"><span>Figure 3—figure supplement 1—source data 1. Data used for generating the graphs in the figure.</span></div>
<div class="media p" id="d64e1125"><div class="caption">
<a href="/articles/instance/10414970/bin/elife-85193-fig3-figsupp1-data1.xlsx" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" class="usa-link">elife-85193-fig3-figsupp1-data1.xlsx</a><sup> (158.6KB, xlsx) </sup>
</div></div></section>
</div></figcaption></figure></div>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/fig3/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></figure><p>We further analyzed the root surface pH response to IAA in line with altered AHA activity. The PIN2&gt;&gt;AHA2-d95 and PIN2&gt;&gt;PP2 CD1 (<a href="#fig2s2" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 2c and d</a>) as well as the <em>pp2c-d triple</em> mutant responded to IAA treatment by alkalinization of the root surface pH (<a href="#fig2s2" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 2h and i</a>). The alkalinization factors (a measure of IAA-induced alkalinization) were similar to Col-0 with the exception of the very root tip of <em>pp2c-d triple</em> which responded slightly less (<a href="#fig2s2" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 2e and j</a>). These results were correlated with control level IAA-induced root growth inhibition (<a href="#fig2s2" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 2b and g</a>). On the other hand, knockout mutation of AHA2 led to reduced IAA-induced root surface alkalinization (<a href="#fig2s2" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 2h, i and j</a>), but strong enough to observe an auxin-induced root growth inhibition (<a href="#fig2s2" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 2g</a>). The alkaline domain does not seem to be caused by the lack of proton efflux in the TZ, and the amplitude of the alkaline domain can be increased by auxin application. We therefore tested how auxin transport, perception, and response contribute to the spatial determination of root surface pH. First, we tested the mutant in the PIN2 auxin efflux carrier, in which the shootward auxin flux through the outer root tissues is perturbed (<a href="#bib40" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib40">Luschnig et al., 1998</a>; <a href="#bib49" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib49">Müller et al., 1998</a>). The <em>pin2</em> mutant root showed a reduced alkaline domain that was shifted toward the root tip (<a href="#fig3" class="usa-link">Figure 3d and e</a>). Upon application of 10 nM IAA to <em>pin2</em> mutant, the alkaline domain position and amplitude were partially restored (<a href="#fig3s1" class="usa-link">Figure 3—figure supplement 1a and b</a>), the <em>pin2</em> roots also responded to auxin by growth inhibition (<a href="#fig3s1" class="usa-link">Figure 3—figure supplement 1c</a>). Upon treatment with 100 nM IAA, the alkaline domain and the overall IAA-induced root alkalinization were fully restored (<a href="#fig3" class="usa-link">Figure 3d and f</a> and <a href="#fig3s1" class="usa-link">Figure 3—figure supplement 1d and e</a>).</p>
<p>The auxin influx carrier AUX1 is essential for auxin uptake and transport during root gravitropism (<a href="#bib72" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib72">Swarup et al., 2005</a>; <a href="#bib2" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib2">Band et al., 2014</a>), and the null <em>aux1</em> mutant was shown to have a more acidic surface with an altered root tip pH profile (<a href="#bib46" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib46">Monshausen et al., 2011</a>). We determined the root surface pH profile in the <em>aux1</em> mutant, and found that its pH showed a gradual decrease from the tip toward the root hair zone. While the acidic zone in the elongation zone was comparable to control roots, <em>aux1</em> mutants displayed a complete absence of the TZ alkaline domain (<a href="#fig3" class="usa-link">Figure 3d and e</a>). Application of 10 nM IAA on <em>aux1</em> mutants did not affect root elongation (<a href="#fig3s1" class="usa-link">Figure 3—figure supplement 1c</a>) and did not trigger a root surface alkalinization in contrast to the control Col-0 (<a href="#fig3s1" class="usa-link">Figure 3—figure supplement 1a and b</a>). However, at 100 nM IAA, when IAA diffusion compensates for AUX1 function (<a href="#bib20" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib20">Evans et al., 1994</a>), <em>aux1</em> mutant showed a clear IAA-induced root growth inhibition (<a href="#fig3s1" class="usa-link">Figure 3—figure supplement 1e</a>) as well as an increased root surface pH beyond the TZ (<a href="#fig3" class="usa-link">Figure 3d and f</a> and <a href="#fig3s1" class="usa-link">Figure 3—figure supplement 1d</a>). The wild-type (WT)-like root surface pH profile was, however, not restored by IAA application, demonstrating that AUX1 is essential for creating the alkaline surface domain in the transition zone.</p>
<p>As the immunolocalization pattern of AHAs in the <em>aux1</em> mutant was comparable to the Col-0 control (<a href="#fig2s1" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 1d</a>), we treated the mutant with FC to investigate the activation status of AHAs in the <em>aux1</em> mutant. Interestingly, this treatment resulted in the establishment of a small alkaline domain also in the <em>aux1</em> mutant (<a href="#fig3s1" class="usa-link">Figure 3—figure supplement 1f and h</a>), likely caused by AHA-mediated acidification in the root tip and the distal elongation zone that did not affect the TZ surface pH. The partial acidification was again correlated with a significant root growth stimulation (<a href="#fig3s1" class="usa-link">Figure 3—figure supplement 1g</a>). This experiment showed that AHAs are not fully activated in <em>aux1</em> mutant and implies a specific surface pH response of the cells of the TZ. In summary, we have shown that the distinctive alkaline domain at the transition zone of the root depends on AUX1-mediated auxin influx. Without auxin influx, the root shows a linear acidification gradient from the root tip to the maturation zone.</p></section><section id="s2-4"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">The components of the rapid auxin response pathway steer the transition zone root surface pH</h3>
<p>Once in the cytoplasm, auxin triggers responses either through the canonical auxin signaling pathway or the auxin rapid response pathway (<a href="#bib17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib17">Dubey et al., 2021</a>). We examined the involvement of these pathways in the establishment of the surface pH profile of the root. First, we investigated the components of the canonical auxin signaling by using the <em>tir1,afb2,3 (tir triple</em>) mutant which lacks the expression of three of the six known auxin receptors (<a href="#bib14" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib14">Dharmasiri et al., 2005</a>). The <em>tir triple</em> mutant displayed a more acidic surface pH, particularly in the elongation and maturation zone (<a href="#fig4" class="usa-link">Figure 4a and b</a>). However, it was still displaying an alkaline domain, albeit less pronounced than the control. In response to IAA, <em>tir triple</em> was critically impaired in whole root IAA-induced surface pH alkalinization (<a href="#fig4s1" class="usa-link">Figure 4—figure supplement 1b and c</a>). As a result, <em>tir triple</em> was also impaired in the IAA-induced root growth inhibition (<a href="#fig4s1" class="usa-link">Figure 4—figure supplement 1a</a>). These results confirm that the canonical auxin signaling is involved in the auxin-induced apoplastic alkalinization (<a href="#bib35" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib35">Li et al., 2021</a>) and also partially in the longitudinal surface pH zonation. Next, to test the role of the TMK1-ABP1 apoplastic auxin perception pathway (<a href="#bib35" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib35">Li et al., 2021</a>; <a href="#bib23" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib23">Friml et al., 2022</a>) in the establishment of root surface pH profile, we analyzed the root surface pH profiles of <em>tmk1</em>, <em>tmk4</em>, and <em>abp1</em> mutants. The surface pH profile of the mutants was, however, comparable to the Col-0 controls (<a href="#fig4s1" class="usa-link">Figure 4—figure supplement 1d, e and f</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="fig4"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 4. Rapid auxin signaling steers root surface pH.</h4>
<figcaption><p>(<strong>a, b</strong>) Root surface pH of Col-0 and <em>tir triple</em> mutant after 20 min treatment with 0 or 10 nM indole-3-acetic acid (IAA). (<strong>c</strong>) Representative images of pH visualization by FS and (<strong>d</strong>) quantification of FS F488/405 excitation ratio. (<strong>e–f</strong>) Root surface pH of Col-0, <em>cngc14-1</em> mutant, and CNGC14::CNGC14-GFP/<em>cngc14-1</em> complemented line after 20 min treatment with 0 or 10 nM IAA. (<strong>e</strong>) Representative images of pH visualization by FS and (<strong>f</strong>) quantification of FS F488/405 excitation ratio. For (b,d,f), the bars above the curves mark regions with non-significant (ns) and significant (*: p-value &lt;0.05) statistical difference in comparison to control. (<strong>h</strong>) Quantification of AFB1-mCitrine and CNGC14-GFP signal intensity in the root epidermis, n=9 (AFB1) and n=15 (CNGC14) roots; error bars = st.dev. The source data can be found in <a href="#fig4sdata1" class="usa-link">Figure 4—source data 1</a>.</p>

<section class="sm xbox font-sm" id="fig4s1sdata1"><div class="caption p"><span>Figure 4—figure supplement 1—source data 1. Data used for generating the graphs in the figure.</span></div>

<div class="p"><figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="fig4s2"><h5 class="obj_head">Figure 4—figure supplement 2. Root surface pH and growth of additional <em>afb1</em> and <em>cngc14</em> mutant alleles.</h5>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=10414970_elife-85193-fig4-figsupp2.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/343a/10414970/6cd711124fc3/elife-85193-fig4-figsupp2.jpg" loading="lazy" height="747" width="749" alt="Figure 4—figure supplement 2."></a></p>
<figcaption><div class="p">(<strong>a</strong>) Comparison of root surface FS F<sub>488/405</sub> excitation ratio profiles of Col-0, <em>afb1-3</em> mutant and AFB1::AFB1-mCitrine/<em>afb1-3</em> complemented line; comparison of genotypes from <a href="#fig4" class="usa-link">Figure 4d</a>. (<strong>b</strong>) Comparison of root surface FS F<sub>488/405</sub> excitation ratio profiles of Col-0, <em>cngc14-1</em> mutant and complemented line CNGC14::CNGC14-GFP/<em>cngc14-1</em>; comparison of genotypes from <a href="#fig4" class="usa-link">Figure 4f</a>. (<strong>c</strong>) Comparison of root surface FS F<sub>488/405</sub> excitation ratio profiles of Col-0, <em>afb1-1s</em> and <em>afb1-3</em> mutants in control conditions. (<strong>d</strong>) Root surface FS F<sub>488/405</sub> excitation ratio profiles of Col-0, <em>cngc14-2</em> mutant and the CNGC14::CNGC14-GFP/<em>cngc14-2</em> complemented line. (<strong>e</strong>) Localization of AUX1-YFP in the indicated mutant lines, scale bar = 50 µm. In (<strong>a–d</strong>), the bars above the curves mark regions with non-significant (ns) and significant (*: p-value &lt;0.05) statistical difference in comparison to control. The source data can be found in <a href="#fig4s2sdata1" class="usa-link">Figure 4—figure supplement 2—source data 1</a>.</div>
<div class="p">
<section class="sm xbox font-sm" id="fig4s2sdata1"><div class="caption p"><span>Figure 4—figure supplement 2—source data 1. Data used for generating the graphs in the figure.</span></div>
<div class="media p" id="d64e1513"><div class="caption">
<a href="/articles/instance/10414970/bin/elife-85193-fig4-figsupp2-data1.xlsx" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" class="usa-link">elife-85193-fig4-figsupp2-data1.xlsx</a><sup> (278.9KB, xlsx) </sup>
</div></div></section>
</div></figcaption></figure></div>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/fig4/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></figure><p>We further explored the role of the recently discovered molecular actors of the auxin rapid response - AFB1 and CNGC14. AFB1 is the paralogue of the TIR1 receptor; AFB1 has been shown to be crucial for the rapid growth inhibition upon auxin treatment, rapid auxin-induced membrane depolarization, and for the early response to gravistimulation (<a href="#bib52" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib52">Prigge et al., 2020</a>; <a href="#bib63" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib63">Serre et al., 2021</a>). Similarly, the mutant in the calcium channel CNGC14 lacks the auxin-induced calcium transient, membrane depolarization, and shows a delay in the early gravitropic response (<a href="#bib66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib66">Shih et al., 2015</a>; <a href="#bib15" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib15">Dindas et al., 2018</a>). We analyzed the root surface pH of both mutants and found a more acidic root surface with a critically flat alkaline domain in the <em>afb1</em> mutant (<a href="#fig4" class="usa-link">Figure 4c and d</a> and <a href="#fig4s2" class="usa-link">Figure 4—figure supplement 2a</a>) as well as in the <em>cngc14</em> mutant (<a href="#fig4" class="usa-link">Figure 4e and f</a> and <a href="#fig4s2" class="usa-link">Figure 4—figure supplement 2b</a>). We confirmed the similar result in additional alleles of <em>afb1</em> (<a href="#fig4s2" class="usa-link">Figure 4—figure supplement 2c</a>) and <em>cngc14</em> mutants (<a href="#fig4s2" class="usa-link">Figure 4—figure supplement 2d</a>). This shows that these proteins, apart from controlling the rapid auxin response, steer the pH profile of the root during normal steady-state growth by controlling the alkaline domain in the transition zone. The lack of the alkaline domain was not caused by mislocalization or absence of AUX1 protein in the <em>afb1</em> or <em>cngc14</em> mutants (<a href="#fig4s2" class="usa-link">Figure 4—figure supplement 2e</a>). Similarly to the <em>aux1</em> mutant, the localization of AHA H<sup>+</sup> ATPases in both mutants was not obviously different from the Col-0 control (<a href="#fig2s1" class="usa-link">Figure 2—figure supplement 1d</a>). We further tested how the <em>afb1</em> and <em>cngc14</em> mutants respond to IAA and found an overall alkalinization of the root surface with a partial rescue of the alkaline domain (<a href="#fig4" class="usa-link">Figure 4d and f</a>) and partially impaired growth inhibition (<a href="#fig4s2" class="usa-link">Figure 4—figure supplement 2f–h</a>).</p>
<p>What makes the TZ zone surface pH different from the other domains of the roots? A plausible explanation is the localization of relevant molecular components in this region of the root. The auxin transporters AUX1 and PIN2 have been shown to localize to the lateral root cap and epidermis (<a href="#bib49" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib49">Müller et al., 1998</a>; <a href="#bib71" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib71">Swarup et al., 2004</a>, <a href="#fig4" class="usa-link">Figure 4g</a>). <a href="#bib52" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib52">Prigge et al., 2020</a>, showed that AFB1 is expressed in the root tip and we could see an enrichment of the protein in the root epidermis (<a href="#fig4" class="usa-link">Figure 4g</a>). To determine the localization of CNGC14, we expressed its fluorescently C-terminally tagged version under the control of its native promoter. The expression of CNGC14 fusion protein was rather weak, and localized to the PM of root epidermal cells, with an enrichment in the transition zone (<a href="#fig4" class="usa-link">Figure 4g and h</a>). The expression of AFB1-mCitrine and CNGC14-GFP in the respective mutants recovered the presence of the TZ alkaline domain (<a href="#fig4" class="usa-link">Figure 4c, d, e and f</a> and <a href="#fig4s2" class="usa-link">Figure 4—figure supplement 2a, b and d</a>). Interestingly, the expression of CNGC14-GFP in <em>cngc14</em> caused an exaggerated TZ alkaline domain (<a href="#fig4" class="usa-link">Figure 4e and f</a> and <a href="#fig4s2" class="usa-link">Figure 4—figure supplement 2b and d</a>), which underlines the importance of the CNGC14 calcium channel in the establishment of root surface pH profile.</p>
<p>These results show that the signaling components so far associated with the rapid auxin response are expressed in the root epidermis and contribute to the longitudinal zonation of root surface pH profile. The alkaline domain of the TZ represents a site of a constant rapid auxin response that is triggered by the internal auxin fluxes mediated by AUX1 and PIN2 transporters. On the other hand, the overall increase of root surface pH upon auxin treatment can be attributed to the TIR1 canonical signaling pathway.</p></section><section id="s2-5"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">The AUX1-AFB1-CNGC14 module facilitates root navigation</h3>
<p>It was shown that auxin induces alkalinization of the apoplastic pH and that this alkalinization correlates and is required for the inhibition of root growth. The same process occurs during the gravitropic response - the lower root side responds to the internal auxin by alkalinization, resulting in root bending (<a href="#bib46" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib46">Monshausen et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#bib66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib66">Shih et al., 2015</a>; <a href="#bib3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib3">Barbez et al., 2017</a>). We reanalyzed the root surface pH using our pH imaging and quantifications, as it enables us to monitor alkalinization, as well as acidification of the root surface. Upon gravistimulation, the TZ alkaline domain on the lower side of the root increased rapidly, while on the upper side, the alkaline domain diminished and the root surface acidified (<a href="#fig5" class="usa-link">Figure 5a</a>, <a href="#video1" class="usa-link">Video 1</a>). This led to a gradient of surface pH across the root that was established within 5 min of the gravistimulation which correlated with initiation of bending of the root (<a href="#fig5" class="usa-link">Figure 5a</a>). The disappearance of the alkaline halo on the upper root surface indicates that the TZ domain might act as a zone of stalled growth which is rapidly activated upon gravistimulation. We further analyzed the gravitropic responses of the mutants with altered root surface pH zonation. As expected, the agravitropic <em>aux1</em> mutant did not create a pH gradient across the root and did not bend (<a href="#fig5" class="usa-link">Figure 5b</a>). Manipulation of the AHA activity by the application of FC or by genetic means did not prevent the formation of a gradient of surface pH and rapid bending of the root (<a href="#fig5s1" class="usa-link">Figure 5—figure supplement 1a and b</a>). Finally, the <em>afb1</em> and <em>cngc14</em> mutants showed a slower gravitropic response, as reported before (<a href="#bib63" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib63">Serre et al., 2021</a>; <a href="#bib66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib66">Shih et al., 2015</a>; <a href="#fig5" class="usa-link">Figure 5c and d</a>). As both mutants have a diminished alkaline domain in the TZ, upon gravistimulation, this zone could not rapidly react to change in auxin fluxes; instead, a shallow gradient of surface pH slowly develops in the mutants (<a href="#fig5" class="usa-link">Figure 5c and d</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="fig5"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 5. Rapid auxin signaling pathway is required for surface alkalinization during rapid gravitropic responses.</h4>
<figcaption><p>(<strong>a–c</strong>) Surface pH dynamics and root tip bending angle during gravitropic response in (<strong>a</strong>) Col-0, (<strong>b</strong>) Col-0 and <em>aux1</em>, (<strong>c</strong>) Col-0 and <em>afb1-3</em>, (<strong>d</strong>) Col-0 and <em>cngc14-1</em> lines. A representative image of pH visualization by FS, quantification of the FS F488/405 excitation ratio of lower/upper root transition zones, and root tip angle dynamics over time are shown for each line. Representative images were taken 40 min after gravistimulation. (<strong>e</strong>) Root surface pH oscillations in vertically growing Col-0 roots. The FS F488/405 excitation ratio images for left and right root sides and their left/right ratio are shown. (<strong>f,g</strong>) Root tip penetration test in 45° inclined media covered with mesh of different pore sizes (109, 119, and 139 µm). (<strong>f</strong>) Schematics of the experimental setup. (<strong>g</strong>) Quantitation of Col-0, <em>aux1</em>, <em>afb1-3</em>, and <em>cngc14-1</em> mesh penetration efficiencies. For (g), statistical differences with p-value &lt;0.05 indicated by *. All scale bars = 100 µm. The source data can be found in <a href="#fig5sdata1" class="usa-link">Figure 5—source data 1</a>.</p>

<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/video1/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></figure><p>We noticed that the alkaline domain showed dynamic fluctuations during vertical growth of the WT root (<a href="#fig5" class="usa-link">Figure 5e</a>, <a href="#video2" class="usa-link">Video 2</a>), similarly to what was described by <a href="#bib46" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="bib46">Monshausen et al., 2011</a>. In the <em>aux1</em>, <em>afb1</em>, and <em>cngc14</em> mutants, the alkaline domain was absent or less prominent during vertical growth (<a href="#video3" class="usa-link">Video 3</a>). This result and the fact that the components of the rapid auxin response pathway are involved in the formation of the alkaline domain indicates that, in this zone, the cells continuously rapidly respond to the internal auxin fluxes, and that this pathway is active not only upon gravistimulation. We hypothesized that the significance of this process is to constantly correct the growth rate fluctuations and to enable the root to quickly regulate the growth direction of the root tip. This would be particularly relevant in the soil environment, where the root tip penetrates the soil particles and constantly corrects growth direction using the gravity vector. To test this hypothesis, we tested the ability of WT and <em>aux1</em>, <em>afb1,</em> and <em>cngc14</em> mutants to navigate through artificial obstacles, approximated by tilted agar plates covered with a rectangular nylon grid. We scored the efficiency of the roots in penetrating the grids with varying pore sizes (<a href="#fig5" class="usa-link">Figure 5f</a>). WT plants penetrated the meshes more efficiently as the pore size increased, whereas the agravitropic <em>aux1</em> mutant failed to penetrate in all conditions. Interestingly, both the <em>afb1</em> and <em>cngc14</em> mutants showed a significant decrease in penetration efficiency compared to the WT (<a href="#fig5" class="usa-link">Figure 5g</a>).</p>
